<p>The <b><i>Virginia</i> class</b>, also known as the <b>SSN-774 class</b>, is a class of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear-powered</a> fast <a href="/wiki/Attack_submarine" title="Attack submarine">attack</a> <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSK_(US_Navy)" title="SSK (US Navy)">hull classification symbol</a> SSN) in service with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a>. The submarines are designed for a broad spectrum of <a href="/wiki/Blue-water_navy" title="Blue-water navy">open-ocean</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">littoral</a> (shallow coastal water) missions. They were conceived as a less expensive alternative to the <a href="/wiki/Seawolf-class_submarine" title="Seawolf-class submarine"><i>Seawolf</i>-class attack submarines</a>, designed during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> era. They are replacing older <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles-class_submarine" title="Los Angeles-class submarine"><i>Los Angeles</i>-classÂ submarines</a>, many of which have already been decommissioned. <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines will be acquired through 2043, and are expected to remain in service past 2060.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> Based on recent updates to the designs, some of the <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines are expected to still be in service in 2070.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes1-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Virginia_class_submarine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1260" data-file-width="2100" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Virginia_class_submarine.jpg/220px-Virginia_class_submarine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Virginia_class_submarine.jpg/330px-Virginia_class_submarine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Virginia_class_submarine.jpg/440px-Virginia_class_submarine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Virginia_class_submarine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Computer-based rendering of Virginia class attack submarine</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The class was developed under the codename Centurion, renamed to New Attack Submarine (NAS) later on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SSN-774_Virginia_class_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-SSN-774_Virginia_class-9">[8]</a></sup> The "Centurion Study" was initiated in February 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines were the first US Navy warships designed with the help of <a href="/wiki/Computer-aided_design" title="Computer-aided design">computer-aided design</a> (CAD) and visualization technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USW_W99_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-USW_W99-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-submarinesuppliers1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-submarinesuppliers1-12">[11]</a></sup> By 2007 approximately 35 million labor hours were spent on designing the <i>Virginia</i> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Around 9 million labor hours are required for the construction and completion of a single <i>Virginia</i>-class submarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-submarinesuppliers1_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-submarinesuppliers1-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navalsubleague1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-navalsubleague1-15">[14]</a></sup> Over 4,000 suppliers are involved in the construction of the <i>Virginia</i> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Each submarine is projected to make 14â15 deployments during its 33-year service life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butler_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Virginia</i> class was intended in part as a less expensive alternative to the <i>Seawolf</i>-class submarines ($1.8 billion vs $2.8 billion), whose production run was stopped after just three boats had been completed. To reduce costs, the <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines use many "<a href="/wiki/Commercial_off-the-shelf" title="Commercial off-the-shelf">commercial off-the-shelf</a>" (COTS) components, especially in their computers and data networks. In practice, they actually cost less than $1.8 billion (in fiscal year 2009 dollars) each, due to improvements in shipbuilding technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker1005_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-baker1005-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In hearings before both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" title="U.S. House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate">Senate</a> committees, the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Research_Service" title="Congressional Research Service">Congressional Research Service</a> (CRS) and expert witnesses testified that the procurement plans of the <i>Virginia</i> class â one per year, accelerating to two per year beginning in 2012 â would result in high unit costs and an insufficient number of attack submarines, according to some of the witnesses and the committee chairman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-fas1-19">[18]</a></sup> In a 10 March 2005 statement to the House Armed Services Committee, Ronald O'Rourke of the CRS testified that, assuming that the production rate remains as planned, "production economies of scale for submarines would continue to remain limited or poor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orourke1_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-orourke1-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, Newport News Shipbuilding and General Dynamics Electric Boat Company built a quarter-scale version of a <i>Virginia</i>-class submarine dubbed Large Scale Vehicle II (LSV II) <i>Cutthroat</i>. The vehicle was designed as an affordable test platform for new technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Virginia</i> class is built through an industrial arrangement designed to keep both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_Boat_Corporation" title="Electric Boat Corporation">GD Electric Boat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newport_News_Shipbuilding" title="Newport News Shipbuilding">Newport News Shipbuilding</a> (the only two U.S. shipyards capable of building nuclear-powered vessels) in the submarine-building business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas3_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-fas3-23">[22]</a></sup> Under the present arrangement, the Newport News facility builds the stern, habitability, and machinery spaces, torpedo room, sail, and bow, while Electric Boat builds the engine room and control room. The facilities alternate work on the reactor plant as well as the final assembly, test, outfit, and delivery.</p>
<p>O'Rourke wrote in 2004 that, "Compared to a one-yard strategy, approaches involving two yards may be more expensive but offer potential offsetting benefits."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RL32418_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-RL32418-1">[1]</a></sup> Among the claims of "offsetting benefits" that O'Rourke attributes to supporters of a two-facility construction arrangement is that it "would permit the United States to continue building submarines at one yard even if the other yard is rendered incapable of building submarines permanently or for a sustained period of time by a catastrophic event of some kind", including an enemy attack.</p>
<p>In order to get the submarine's price down to $2 billion per submarine in FY-05 dollars, the Navy instituted a cost-reduction program to shave off approximately $400 million of each submarine's price tag. The project was dubbed "2 for 4 in 12," referring to the Navy's desire to buy two boats for $4Â billion in FY-12. Under pressure from Congress, the Navy opted to start buying two boats per year in FY-11, meaning that officials would not be able to get the $2Â billion price tag before the service started buying two submarines per year. However, program manager Dave Johnson said at a conference on 19 March 2008 that the program was only $30Â million away from achieving the $2Â billion price goal, and would reach that target on schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Virginia Class Program Office received the David Packard Excellence in Acquisition Award in 1996, 1998, 2008, "for excelling in four specific award criteria: reducing life-cycle costs; making the acquisition system more efficient, responsive, and timely; integrating defense with the commercial base and practices; and promoting continuous improvement of the acquisition process".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2008, the Navy signed a $14 billion contract with General Dynamics and Northrop Grumman to supply eight submarines. The contractors will deliver one submarine in each of fiscal 2009 and 2010, and two submarines on each of fiscal 2011, 2012, and 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> This contract will bring the Navy's <i>Virginia</i>-class fleet to 18 submarines. And in December 2010, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> passed a defense authorization bill that expanded production to two subs per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Two submarine-per-year production resumed on 2 September 2011 with commencement of <a href="/wiki/USS_Washington_(SSN-787)" title="USS Washington (SSN-787)"><i>Washington</i>Â (SSN-787)</a> construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssn787_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ssn787-3">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 21 June 2008, the Navy christened <a href="/wiki/USS_New_Hampshire_(SSN-778)" title="USS New Hampshire (SSN-778)"><i>New Hampshire</i>Â (SSN-778)</a>, the first Block II submarine. This boat was delivered eight months ahead of schedule and $54Â million under budget. BlockÂ II boats are built in four sections, compared to the ten sections of the Block I boats. This enables a cost saving of about $300 million per boat, reducing the overall cost to $2 billion per boat and the construction of two new boats per year. Beginning in 2010, new submarines of this class will include a software system that can monitor and reduce their <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetic</a> signatures when needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first full-duration six-month deployment was successfully carried out from 15 October 2009 to 13 April 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Authorization of full-rate production and the declaration of full operational capability was achieved five months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> In September 2010, it was found that urethane tiles, applied to the hull to damp internal sound and absorb rather than reflect sonar pulses, were falling off while the subs were at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> Admiral Kevin McCoy announced that the problems with the Mold-in-Place Special Hull Treatment for the early subs had been fixed in 2011, then <i>Minnesota</i> was built and found to have the same problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Professor_Ross_Babbage" title="Professor Ross Babbage">Professor Ross Babbage</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_National_University" title="Australian National University">Australian National University</a> has called on Australia to buy or lease a dozen <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines from the United States, rather than locally build <a href="/wiki/Collins-class_submarine_replacement_project" title="Collins-class submarine replacement project">12 replacements</a> for its <a href="/wiki/Collins-class_submarine" title="Collins-class submarine"><i>Collins</i>-classÂ submarines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, just as two-per-year sub construction was supposed to commence, Congress failed to resolve the <a href="/wiki/United_States_fiscal_cliff" title="United States fiscal cliff">United States fiscal cliff</a>, forcing the Navy to attempt to "de-obligate" construction funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Innovations">Innovations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Innovations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Virginia</i> class incorporates several innovations not found in previous US submarine classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker1005_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-baker1005-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology_barriers">Technology barriers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Technology barriers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of the low rate of <i>Virginia</i> production, the Navy entered into a program with <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">DARPA</a> to overcome technology barriers to lower the cost of attack submarines so that more could be built, to maintain the size of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>These include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Propulsion concepts not constrained by a centerline shaft.</li>
<li>Externally stowed and launched weapons (especially torpedoes).</li>
<li>Conformal alternatives to the existing spherical sonar array.</li>
<li>Technologies that eliminate or substantially simplify existing submarine hull, mechanical, and electrical systems.</li>
<li>Automation to reduce crew workload for standard tasks</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unified_Modular_Masts">Unified Modular Masts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Unified Modular Masts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For the first time in history the design of the eight <a href="/wiki/Radio_masts_and_towers" title="Radio masts and towers">masts</a> in Virginia class has been unified in a Universal Modular Mast (UMM) program led by L-3 KEO<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> (previously Kollmorgen):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> shared components have been maximised and some design choices are also shared between different masts. The first UMM was first installed on <a href="/wiki/USS_Memphis_(SSN-691)" title="USS Memphis (SSN-691)">USSÂ <i>Memphis</i></a>, a <i>Los Angeles</i>-class submarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The UMM is an integrated system for housing, erecting, and supporting submarine mast-mounted antennas and sensors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> The UMMs are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapower-digital1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-seapower-digital1-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>snorkel mast</li>
<li>two photonic masts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapower-digital1_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-seapower-digital1-42">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>two (tactical) communication masts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapower-digital1_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-seapower-digital1-42">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>one or two high-data-rate satellite communication (SATCOM) masts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> built by Raytheon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raytheon1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-raytheon1-44">[43]</a></sup> enabling communication at Super High Frequency (for downlink) and Extremely High Frequency (for uplink) range<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raytheon1_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-raytheon1-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li>radar mast (carrying AN/BPS-16 surface search and navigation radar)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>electronic warfare mast (AN/BLQ-10 Electronic Support Measures) used to detect, analyze, and identify both radar and communication signals from ships, aircraft, submarines, and land-based transmitters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photonics_masts">Photonics masts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Photonics masts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Virginia</i> class is the first to utilize photonic sensors instead of a traditional <a href="/wiki/Periscope" title="Periscope">periscope</a>: the class is equipped with high-<a href="/wiki/Image_resolution" title="Image resolution">resolution</a> cameras, along with light-intensification and <a href="/wiki/Thermographic_camera" title="Thermographic camera">infrared sensors</a>, an infrared <a href="/wiki/Laser_rangefinder" title="Laser rangefinder">laser rangefinder</a>, and an integrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_Support_Measures" title="Electronic Support Measures">Electronic Support Measures</a> (ESM) array. Two redundant sets of these sensors are mounted on two <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=AN/BVS-1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="AN/BVS-1 (page does not exist)">AN/BVS-1</a> <a href="/wiki/Photonics_mast" title="Photonics mast">photonics masts</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker1005_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-baker1005-18">[17]</a></sup> located outside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_hull" title="Pressure hull">pressure hull</a>. Signals from the masts' sensors are transmitted through <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fiber</a> data lines through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_processor" title="Signal processor">signal processors</a> to the control center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Visual feeds from the masts are displayed on <a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">liquid-crystal display</a> interfaces in the command center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USW_W99_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-USW_W99-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The design of earlier optical periscopes required them to penetrate the pressure hull, reducing the structural integrity of the pressure hull as well as increasing the risk of flooding, and also required the submarine's control room to be located directly below the sail/fin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Implementation of photonics masts (which do not penetrate the pressure hull) enabled the submarine control room to be relocated to a position inside the pressure hull which is not necessarily directly below the sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapower-digital1_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-seapower-digital1-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The current photonics masts have a visual appearance so different from the ordinary periscopes that when the submarine is detected, it can be distinctly identified as a <i>Virginia</i>-class vessel. As a result, current photonic masts will be replaced with Low-Profile Photonics Masts (LPPM) which resemble traditional submarine periscopes more closely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapower-digital1_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-seapower-digital1-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the future, a non-rotational Affordable Modular Panoramic Photonics Mast may be fitted, enabling the submarine to obtain a simultaneous 360Â° view of the sea surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsor">Propulsor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Propulsor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contrast to a traditional bladed propeller, the <i>Virginia</i> class uses <a href="/wiki/Pump-jet" title="Pump-jet">pump-jet</a> propulsors (built by <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems" title="BAE Systems">BAE Systems</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> originally developed for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Swiftsure-class_submarine" title="Swiftsure-class submarine"><i>Swiftsure</i>-classÂ submarines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> The propulsor significantly reduces the risks of <a href="/wiki/Cavitation" title="Cavitation">cavitation</a>, and allows quieter operation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improved_sonar_systems">Improved sonar systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Improved sonar systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">Sonar</a> arrays aboard <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines have an "<a href="/wiki/Open_system_(computing)" title="Open system (computing)">Open System Architecture</a>" (OSA) which enables rapid insertion of new hardware and software as they become available. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardware_upgrade" title="Hardware upgrade">Hardware upgrades</a> (dubbed Technology Insertions) are usually carried out every four years, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_update" title="Software update">software updates</a> (dubbed Advanced Processor Builds) are carried out every two years. <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines feature several types of sonar arrays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npg13_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-npg13-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>BQQ-10 bow-mounted spherical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_sonar" title="Active sonar">active</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_sonar" title="Passive sonar">passive</a> sonar array<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npg13_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-npg13-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> (Large Aperture Bow (LAB) sonar array from SSN-784 onwards)</li>
<li>A wide aperture lightweight fiber optic sonar array, consisting of three flat panels mounted low along either side of the hull<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two high frequency active sonars mounted in the sail and bow. The chin-mounted (below the bow) and sail-mounted high frequency sonars supplement the (spherical/LAB) main sonar array, enabling safer operations in coastal waters, enhancing under-ice navigation, and improving <a href="/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare" title="Anti-submarine warfare">anti-submarine warfare</a> performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Low-Cost <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Conformal" title="wikt:Conformal">Conformal</a> Array (LCCA) <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">high frequency</a> sonar, mounted on both sides of the submarine's sail. Provides coverage above and behind the submarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p><i>Virginia</i>-class submarines are also equipped with a low frequency <a href="/wiki/Towed_array_sonar" title="Towed array sonar">towed sonar array</a> and a high frequency towed sonar array.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-military.com_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-military.com-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>TB-16 or TB-34 fat line tactical towed sonar array<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globalsecurity1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-globalsecurity1-64">[63]</a></sup></li>
<li>TB-29 or TB-33 thin line long-range search towed sonar array<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globalsecurity1_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-globalsecurity1-64">[63]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rescue_equipment">Rescue equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rescue equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>SEIE MK11 suit(s) â enable ascent from a sunken submarine (maximum ascent depth 600 feet)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npg13_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-npg13-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydroxide" title="Lithium hydroxide">lithium hydroxide</a> canisters that remove <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the submarine's atmosphere<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npg13_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-npg13-56">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>Submarine Emergency Position Indicating Radio Beacon (SEPIRB)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginia_Payload_Module">Virginia Payload Module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Virginia Payload Module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_(SSN-785)_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2891" data-file-width="4355" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_%28SSN-785%29_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg/220px-Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_%28SSN-785%29_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_%28SSN-785%29_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg/330px-Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_%28SSN-785%29_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_%28SSN-785%29_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg/440px-Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_%28SSN-785%29_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Commissioning_of_USS_John_Warner_(SSN-785)_-_Manning_the_rails.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_John_Warner_(SSN-785)" title="USS John Warner (SSN-785)">USSÂ <i>John Warner</i></a> alongside, with forward Virginia Payload Tube hatch open</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Block III submarines have two multipurpose Virginia Payload Tubes (VPT) replacing the dozen single purpose cruise missile launch tubes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Block V submarines built from 2019 onward will have an additional Virginia Payload Module (VPM) mid-body section, increasing their overall length. The VPM will add four more VPTs of the same diameter and greater height, located on the centerline, carrying up to seven Tomahawk missiles apiece, that would replace some of the capabilities lost when the SSGN conversion <a href="/wiki/Ohio-class_submarine" title="Ohio-class submarine"><i>Ohio</i>-classÂ submarines</a> are retired from the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RL32418_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-RL32418-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VPM_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-VPM-69">[68]</a></sup> Initially eight payload tubes/silos were planned<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VPM_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-VPM-69">[68]</a></sup> but this was later rejected in favour of 4 tubes installed in a 70-foot long module between the operations compartment and the propulsion spaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VPM_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-VPM-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The VPM could potentially carry (non-nuclear) medium-range ballistic missiles. Adding the VPM would increase the cost of each submarine by $500 million (2012 prices).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> This additional cost would be offset by reducing the total submarine force by four ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> More recent reports state that as a cost reduction measure the VPM would carry only Tomahawk SLCM and possibly unmanned undersea vehicles (UUV) with the new price tag now estimated at $360â380 million per boat (in 2010 prices). The VPM launch tubes/silos will reportedly be similar in design to the ones planned for the <i>Ohio</i> class replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> As of September 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Operations" title="Chief of Naval Operations">Chief of Naval Operations</a> (CNO) was still hoping to field the VPM from 2027,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenert20130918_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenert20130918-76">[75]</a></sup> but deployment now seems unlikely since the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Requirements_Oversight_Council" title="Joint Requirements Oversight Council">Joint Requirements Oversight Council</a> (JROC) moved the program in February 2013 from the 'Prompt Strike' budget to the main Navy shipbuilding account, which is already under financial pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tright">

<li><a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fiber</a> <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> <b>Ship Control System</b> replaces electro-hydraulic systems for control surface actuation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Command_and_control" title="Command and control">Command and control</a> system module (CCSM) built by Lockheed Martin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naval-TechNSSN_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naval-TechNSSN-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>Modernized version of the AN/BSY-1 integrated combat system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SSN-774_Virginia_class_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-SSN-774_Virginia_class-9">[8]</a></sup> designated AN/BYG-1 (previously designated CCS Mk2) and built by <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics" title="General Dynamics">General Dynamics</a> AIS (previously <a href="/wiki/Raytheon" title="Raytheon">Raytheon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> AN/BYG-1 integrates the submarine Tactical Control System (TCS) and Weapon Control System (WCS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/USS_California_(SSN-781)" title="USS California (SSN-781)">USSÂ <i>California</i></a> was the first <i>Virginia</i>-class submarine with the advanced electromagnetic signature reduction system built into it, but this system is being retrofitted into the other submarines of the class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Integral 9-man lock-out chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aticourses_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-aticourses-84">[83]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<li>Builders: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_Boat_Corporation" title="Electric Boat Corporation">GD Electric Boat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huntington_Ingalls_Industries" title="Huntington Ingalls Industries">HII Newport News Shipbuilding</a></li>
<li>Length: 377Â ft (114.91Â m)</li>
<li>Beam: 34Â ft (10.36Â m)</li>
<li>Displacement: 7,800 long tons (7,900Â t)</li>
<li>Payload: 40 weapons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_operations_force" title="Special operations force">special operations forces</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unmanned_undersea_vehicle" title="Unmanned undersea vehicle">unmanned undersea vehicles</a>, Advanced <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_SEALs" title="United States Navy SEALs">SEAL</a> Delivery System (<a href="/wiki/Advanced_SEAL_Delivery_System" title="Advanced SEAL Delivery System">ASDS</a>)</li>
<li>Propulsion: The <a href="/wiki/S9G_reactor" title="S9G reactor">S9G nuclear reactor</a> delivering 40,000 shaft horse power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Nuclear core life estimated at 33 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>Maximum diving depth: greater than 800Â ft (240Â m), allegedly around 1,600 feet (490Â m)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aticourses_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-aticourses-84">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li>Speed: Greater than 25 knots (46Â km/h; 29Â mph),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NavyFactFile_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-NavyFactFile-87">[86]</a></sup> allegedly up to 34 knots (63Â km/h; 39Â mph)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aticourses_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-aticourses-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>Planned cost: about US$1.65 billion each (based on FY95 dollars, 30-ship class and two ship/year build-rate)</li>
<li>Actual cost: US$1.5 billion (in 1994 prices), US$2.6 billion (in 2012 prices)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li>Annual operating cost: $50 million per unit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>Crew: 120 enlisted and 14 officers</li>
<li>Armament: 12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical_Launching_System" title="Vertical Launching System">VLS</a> &amp; four <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a> tubes, capable of launching <a href="/wiki/Mark_48_torpedo" title="Mark 48 torpedo">Mark 48 torpedoes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tactical_Tomahawk" title="Tactical Tomahawk">UGM-109 Tactical Tomahawks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harpoon_(missile)" title="Harpoon (missile)">Harpoon (missile)s</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> and the new advanced mobile <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">mine</a> when it becomes available.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Decoys: Acoustic Device Countermeasure Mk 3/4<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Boats">Boats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Boats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Block_I">Block I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Block I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modular construction techniques were incorporated during construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Earlier submarines (e.g., <i>Los Angeles</i>-class SSNs) were built by assembling the pressure hull and then installing the equipment via cavities in the pressure hull. This required extensive construction activities within the narrow confines of the pressure hull which was time-consuming and dangerous. Modular construction was implemented in an effort to overcome these problems and make the construction process more efficient. Modular construction techniques incorporated during construction include constructing large segments of equipment outside the hull. These segments (dubbed rafts) are then inserted into a hull section (a large segment of the pressure hull). The integrated raft and hull section form a module which, when joined with other modules, forms a <i>Virginia</i>-class submarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmander_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmander-95">[94]</a></sup> Block I boats were built in 10 modules with each submarine requiring roughly 7 years (84 months) to build.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Block_II">Block II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Block II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Block II boats were built in four sections rather than ten sections, saving about $300 million per boat. Block II boats (excluding SSN-778) were also built under a multi-year procurement agreement as opposed to a block-buy contract in Block I, enabling savings in the range of $400 million ($80 million per boat).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RL32418_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-RL32418-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butler_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-17">[16]</a></sup> As a result of improvements in the construction process, <i>New Hampshire</i> was 500 million USD cheaper, required 3.7 million fewer labor hours to build (25% less) thus shortening the construction period by 15 months (20% less) compared to <i>Virginia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmander_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holmander-95">[94]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Block_III">Block III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Block III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="960" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG/220px-USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG/330px-USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG/440px-USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)_at_sea_in_August_2014.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)" title="USS North Dakota (SSN-784)"><i>North Dakota</i></a> is first of the VPT-equipped Block III <i>Virginia</i>s</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="960" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG/220px-USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG/330px-USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG/440px-USS_North_Dakota_%28SSN-784%29_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)_overhead_photo_in_August_2014.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Overhead view of the Block III <a href="/wiki/USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)" title="USS North Dakota (SSN-784)"><i>North Dakota</i></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/USS_North_Dakota_(SSN-784)" title="USS North Dakota (SSN-784)">SSN-784</a> through approximately <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSN-791" title="SSN-791">SSN-791</a> are planned to make up the Third Block or "Flight" and began construction in 2009. Block III subs feature a revised bow with a Large Aperture Bow (LAB) sonar array, as well as technology from <i>Ohio</i>-class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSGN" title="SSGN">SSGNs</a> (2 VLS tubes each containing 6 missiles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RevisedBow_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-RevisedBow-109">[108]</a></sup> The horseshoe-shaped LAB sonar array replaces the spherical main sonar array which has been used on all U.S. Navy SSNs since 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butler_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> The LAB sonar array is water-backedâas opposed to earlier sonar arrays which were air-backedâand consists of a passive array and a medium-frequency active array.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> Compared to earlier <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines about 40% of the bow has been redesigned.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>South Dakota</i> (SSN-790) will be equipped with a new propulsor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapowermagazine.org_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-seapowermagazine.org-114">[113]</a></sup> possibly the Hybrid Multi-Material Rotor (HMMR),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darpa.mil_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-darpa.mil-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summit-group.com_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-summit-group.com-116">[115]</a></sup> developed by Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency (DARPA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapowermagazine.org_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-seapowermagazine.org-114">[113]</a></sup> The Hybrid Multi-Material Rotor program is an attempt to improve the design and manufacturing process of submarine propellers with an aim of reducing the cost and weight of the propeller/rotor as well as improving overall acoustic performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seapowermagazine.org_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-seapowermagazine.org-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darpa.mil_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-darpa.mil-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summit-group.com_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-summit-group.com-116">[115]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Block_IV">Block IV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Block IV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2013 execution of a 10-submarine contract was put in doubt by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Budget_sequestration_in_2013" title="Budget sequestration in 2013">Budget sequestration in 2013</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> The most costly shipbuilding contract in history was awarded on 28 April 2014 as prime contractor General Dynamics Electric Boat took on a $17.6 billion contract for ten Block IV <i>Virginia</i>-class attack submarines. The main improvement over the Block III is the reduction of major maintenance periods from four to three, increasing each ship's total lifetime deployments by one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The long-lead-time materials contract for SSN 792 was awarded on 17 April 2012, with SSN 793 and SSN 794 following on 28 December 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> the U.S. Navy has awarded General Dynamics Electric Boat a $208.6 million contract modification for the second <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> (FY) 14 <i>Virginia</i>-class submarine, SSN-793, and two FY 15 submarines, SSN-794 and SSN-795. With this modification, the overall contract is worth $595 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> Block IV consists of 10 submarines and comprises the following hull numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Block_V">Block V</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Block V">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Block V subs may incorporate the Virginia Payload Module (VPM), which would give guided-missile capability when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSGN" title="SSGN">SSGNs</a> are retired from service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> The Block V subs are expected to triple the capacity of shore targets for each boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-forbes1-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-802&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-802 (page does not exist)">SSN-802</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-803&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-803 (page does not exist)">SSN-803</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-804&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-804 (page does not exist)">SSN-804</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-805&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-805 (page does not exist)">SSN-805</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-806&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-806 (page does not exist)">SSN-806</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-807&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-807 (page does not exist)">SSN-807</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SSN-808&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SSN-808 (page does not exist)">SSN-808</a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_acquisitions">Future acquisitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Future acquisitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Navy plans to acquire at least 30 <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jeffhead1_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-jeffhead1-149">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-submarinesuppliers2_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-submarinesuppliers2-150">[149]</a></sup> however, more recent data provided by the Naval Submarine League (in 2011) and the Congressional Budget Office (in 2012) seems to imply that more than 30 may eventually be built. The Naval Submarine League believes that up to 10Â BlockÂ V boats will be built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navalsubleague1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-navalsubleague1-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> The same source also states that 10 additional submarines could be built after BlockÂ V submarines, with 5 in the so-called BlockÂ VI and 5 in BlockÂ VII, largely due to the delays experienced with the "Improved <i>Virginia</i>". These 20Â submarines (10Â BlockÂ V, 5Â BlockÂ VI, 5Â BlockÂ VII) would carry VPM bringing the total number of <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines to 48 (including the 28Â submarines in Blocks I, II, III and IV). The CBO in its 2012 report states that 33Â <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines will be procured in the 2013â2032 timeframe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbo_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-cbo-5">[4]</a></sup> resulting in 49Â submarines in total since 16 were already procured by the end of 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAS_Weapons_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAS_Weapons-152">[151]</a></sup> Such a long production run seems unlikely but it should be noted that another naval program, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arleigh_Burke_class_destroyer" title="Arleigh Burke class destroyer"><i>Arleigh Burke</i>-classÂ destroyer</a>, is still ongoing even though the first vessel was procured in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> However, other sources believe that production will end with BlockÂ V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> In addition, data provided in CBO reports tends to vary considerably compared to earlier editions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbo_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-cbo-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="SSN.28X.29.2FImproved_Virginia">SSN(X)/Improved <i>Virginia</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: SSN(X)/Improved Virginia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Initially dubbed Future Attack Submarine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> and Improved <i>Virginia</i> class in Congressional Budget Office (CBO) reports,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbo_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-cbo-5">[4]</a></sup> the SSN(X) or Improved <i>Virginia</i>-class submarines will be an evolved version of the <i>Virginia</i> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbo_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-cbo-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 2014, the Navy began early preparation work on the SSN(X). It was planned that the first submarine would be procured in 2025. However, their introduction (i.e., procurement of the first submarine) has been pushed back to 2033/2034.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbo_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-cbo-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> The long-range shipbuilding plan is for the new SSN to be authorized in 2034, and become operational by 2044 after the last Block VII <i>Virginia</i> is built. Roughly a decade will be spent identifying, designing, and demonstrating new technologies before an analysis of alternatives is issued in 2024. An initial small team has been formed to consult with industry and identify the threat environment and technologies the submarine will need to operate against in the 2050-plus timeframe. One area already identified is the need to integrate with off-board systems so future <i>Virginia</i> boats and the SSN(X) can employ networked, extremely long-ranged weapons. A torpedo propulsion system concept from the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a> could allow a torpedo to hit a target 200Â nmi (230Â mi; 370Â km) away and be guided by another asset during the terminal phase. Targeting information might also come from another platform like a patrol aircraft or an <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">unmanned aerial vehicle</a> (UAV) launched from the submarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> Researchers have identified a quieter advanced propulsion system and the ability to control multiple <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_underwater_vehicle" title="Unmanned underwater vehicle">unmanned underwater vehicles</a> (UUVs) at once as key SSN(X) components. The future submarines will operate through the end of the 21st century, and potentially into the 22nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> New propulsion technology, moving beyond the use of a rotating mechanical device to push the ship through the water, could come in the form a <a href="/wiki/Biomimetics" title="Biomimetics">biomimetic</a> propulsion system that would eliminate noise-generating moving parts like the drive shaft and the spinning blades of the propulsor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Humor">In Humor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virginia-class_submarine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In Humor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Beginning in 2015, <a href="/wiki/Dan%27s_Papers" title="Dan's Papers">Dan's Papers</a>, a weekly lifestyle publication in the Hamptons area of Long Island, New York, initiated satirical news spoofs featuring the "Hamptons Police Submarine Unit" that caricaturized the affluent area's police department acquisition of a "military surplus" Virginia class submarine for routine ocean patrols and law enforcement, with similar tongue-in-cheek overtones of excessive <a href="/wiki/Militarization_of_police" title="Militarization of police">militarization of police</a>. While perhaps clearly a spoof to an informed reader, the news reports were otherwise realistic and of consequent readership response that the paper later featured a spoof local investigation titled "Area Man Reveals Hamptons Police Submarine Story as Hoax."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> The satire was also of sufficient quality to eventually lead to the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Snopes.com" title="Snopes.com">Snopes.com</a> refutation of the existence of such an expensive ($2.688 Billion) military surplus acquisition for local police department usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup></p>
